     <p><b>Dartmouth College</b> (commonly referred to as <b>Dartmouth</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/?/ primary stress follows" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">?</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'d' in 'dye'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">d</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/?r/ 'ar' in 'bard'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">?r</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'t' in 'tie'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">t</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'m' in 'my'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">m</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/?/ 'a' in 'about'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">?</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/?/ 'th' in 'thigh'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">?</span></a></span><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling" class="Unicode"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Wikipedia:Pronunciation respelling key"><i><b><span class="smallcaps"><span class="SMALLCAPS" style="FONT-VARIANT:SMALL-CAPS;"><span class="NOCAPS" style="TEXT-TRANSFORM:LOWERCASE;">DART</span></span></span></b>-m?th</i></a></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Private_university" title="Private university">private</a> <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> <a href="/wiki/Doctoral_university" title="Doctoral university" class="mw-redirect">research university</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Hanover,_New_Hampshire" title="Hanover, New Hampshire">Hanover</a>, New Hampshire, United States. The institution consists of a liberal arts college, the <a href="/wiki/Geisel_School_of_Medicine" title="Geisel School of Medicine">Geisel School of Medicine</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Thayer_School_of_Engineering" title="Thayer School of Engineering">Thayer School of Engineering</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Tuck_School_of_Business" title="Tuck School of Business">Tuck School of Business</a>, as well as 19 graduate programs in the arts and sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-usnwr-aag_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usnwr-aag-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Incorporated as the &quot;Trustees of Dartmouth College,&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-charter_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-charter-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> it is one of the nine <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Colleges" title="Colonial Colleges" class="mw-redirect">Colonial Colleges</a> founded before the American Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-about-dartmouth-facts_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-about-dartmouth-facts-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> With an undergraduate enrollment of 4,194 and a total student enrollment of 6,144, Dartmouth is the smallest university in the Ivy League.<sup id="cite_ref-About_Dartmouth_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-About_Dartmouth-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Dartmouth College was established in 1769 by <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregational</a> minister <a href="/wiki/Eleazar_Wheelock" title="Eleazar Wheelock">Eleazar Wheelock</a>. After a long period of financial and political struggles, Dartmouth emerged in the early 20th century from relative obscurity.<sup id="cite_ref-trd-wheelock_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trd-wheelock-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Dartmouth alumni, from <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Webster" title="Daniel Webster">Daniel Webster</a> to the many donors in the 19th and 20th centuries, have been famously involved in their college.<sup id="cite_ref-ihe-alum_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ihe-alum-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Dartmouth is located on a rural 269 acres (1.09&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) campus in the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Valley_(Connecticut_River)" title="Upper Valley (Connecticut River)" class="mw-redirect">Upper Valley</a> region of New Hampshire. The campus is isolated, and participation in athletics and the school's <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College_Greek_organizations" title="Dartmouth College Greek organizations">Greek system</a> is strong.<sup id="cite_ref-abc-greek_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abc-greek-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Dartmouth's 34 varsity sports teams compete in the <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> conference of the <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Athletic_Association" title="National Collegiate Athletic Association">NCAA</a> Division I. Students are well known for preserving a variety of strong campus traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-A_Frat_Party_Is:.3B_a.29_Milk_and_Cookies.3B_b.29_Beer_Pong_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Frat_Party_Is:.3B_a.29_Milk_and_Cookies.3B_b.29_Beer_Pong-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hill_Winds.2C_Granite_Brains.2C_and_Other_Dartmouth_Traditions_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hill_Winds.2C_Granite_Brains.2C_and_Other_Dartmouth_Traditions-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Our_Mission_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Our_Mission-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dartmouth:_Forever_New_An_address_by_President_James_Wright:_On_the_Occasion_of_his_Inauguration_as_the_16th_President_of_Dartmouth_College_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth:_Forever_New_An_address_by_President_James_Wright:_On_the_Occasion_of_his_Inauguration_as_the_16th_President_of_Dartmouth_College-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <table id="toc" class="toc"> 
      <tbody>
       <tr>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Dartmouth_College&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_presidents_of_Dartmouth_College" title="List of presidents of Dartmouth College">List of presidents of Dartmouth College</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Dartmouth was founded by <a href="/wiki/Eleazar_Wheelock" title="Eleazar Wheelock">Eleazar Wheelock</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Puritan" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> minister from Columbia, Connecticut, who had previously sought to establish a school to train Native Americans as missionaries. Wheelock's ostensible inspiration for such an establishment resulted from his relationship with <a href="/wiki/Mohegan" title="Mohegan" class="mw-redirect">Mohegan</a> Indian <a href="/wiki/Samson_Occom" title="Samson Occom">Samson Occom</a>. Occom became an ordained minister after studying under Wheelock from 1743 to 1747, and later moved to <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a> to preach to the Montauks.<sup id="cite_ref-history-lesson_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-lesson-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Wheelock founded <a href="/wiki/Moor%27s_Charity_School" title="Moor's Charity School">Moor's Indian Charity School in 1755</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Eleazar_Wheelock.27s_Two_Schools_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eleazar_Wheelock.27s_Two_Schools-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> The Charity School proved somewhat successful, but additional funding was necessary to continue school's operations, and Wheelock sought the help of friends to raise money. Occom, accompanied by the Reverend Nathaniel Whitaker, traveled to England in 1766 to raise money from churches. With these funds, they established a trust to help Wheelock.<sup id="cite_ref-history-lesson_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-lesson-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> The head of the trust was a <a href="/wiki/Methodist" title="Methodist" class="mw-redirect">Methodist</a> named <a href="/wiki/William_Legge,_2nd_Earl_of_Dartmouth" title="William Legge, 2nd Earl of Dartmouth">William Legge, 2nd Earl of Dartmouth</a>.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-02_03_-_Charter.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-02_03_-_Charter.jpg/170px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-02_03_-_Charter.jpg" width="170" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-02_03_-_Charter.jpg/255px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-02_03_-_Charter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-02_03_-_Charter.jpg/340px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-02_03_-_Charter.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-02_03_-_Charter.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Charter of Dartmouth College on display in 
        <a href="/wiki/Baker_Memorial_Library" title="Baker Memorial Library">Baker Memorial Library</a>. The Charter was signed on December 13, 1769, on behalf of 
        <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">King George III of Great Britain</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Although the fund provided Wheelock ample financial support for the Charity School, Wheelock had trouble recruiting Indians to the institution, primarily because its location was far from tribal territories. In seeking to expand the school into a college, Wheelock relocated it to Hanover, in the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_New_Hampshire" title="Province of New Hampshire">Province of New Hampshire</a>. The move from Connecticut followed a lengthy and sometimes frustrating effort to find resources and secure a charter. The Royal Governor of New Hampshire, <a href="/wiki/John_Wentworth_(governor)" title="John Wentworth (governor)" class="mw-redirect">John Wentworth</a>, provided the land upon which Dartmouth would be built and on December 13, 1769, issued the charter in the name of <a href="/wiki/King_George_III" title="King George III" class="mw-redirect">King George III</a> establishing the College. That charter created a college &quot;for the education and instruction of Youth of the Indian Tribes in this Land ... and also of English Youth and any others.&quot; The reference to educating Native American youth was included to connect Dartmouth to the Charity School and enable use of the Charity School's unspent trust funds. Named for <a href="/wiki/William_Legge,_2nd_Earl_of_Dartmouth" title="William Legge, 2nd Earl of Dartmouth">William Legge, 2nd Earl of Dartmouth</a>?an important supporter of Eleazar Wheelock's earlier efforts but who, in fact, opposed creation of the College and never donated to it?Dartmouth is the nation's ninth oldest college and the last institution of higher learning established under Colonial rule.<sup id="cite_ref-About_Dartmouth.C2.A0.E2.80.93_History_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-About_Dartmouth.C2.A0.E2.80.93_History-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> The College granted its first degrees in 1771.<sup id="cite_ref-Eleazar_Wheelock_and_the_Adventurous_Founding_of_Dartmouth_College_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eleazar_Wheelock_and_the_Adventurous_Founding_of_Dartmouth_College-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Given the limited success of the Charity School, however, Wheelock intended his new College as one primarily for whites.<sup id="cite_ref-history-lesson_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-lesson-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-chi_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chi-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Occom, disappointed with Wheelock's departure from the school's original goal of Indian Christianization, went on to form his own community of New England Indians called <a href="/wiki/Brothertown_Indians" title="Brothertown Indians">Brothertown Indians</a> in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-history-lesson_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-lesson-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-chi_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chi-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Early_Dartmouth_Dunham.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Early_Dartmouth_Dunham.jpg/220px-Early_Dartmouth_Dunham.jpg" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Early_Dartmouth_Dunham.jpg/330px-Early_Dartmouth_Dunham.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Early_Dartmouth_Dunham.jpg/440px-Early_Dartmouth_Dunham.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Early_Dartmouth_Dunham.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The earliest known image of Dartmouth appeared in the February 1793 issue of 
        <i>Massachusetts Magazine</i>. The engraving may also be the first visual proof of 
        <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a> being played in the United States.
        <sup id="cite_ref-CricketRauner_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CricketRauner-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In 1819, Dartmouth College was the subject of the historic <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College_v._Woodward" title="Dartmouth College v. Woodward">Dartmouth College case</a>, which challenged <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>'s 1816 attempt to amend the College's royal charter to make the school a public university. An institution called <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_University" title="Dartmouth University">Dartmouth University</a> occupied the College buildings and began operating in Hanover in 1817, though the College continued teaching classes in rented rooms nearby.<sup id="cite_ref-history-lesson_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-lesson-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Webster" title="Daniel Webster">Daniel Webster</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Alumnus" title="Alumnus">alumnus</a> of the class of 1801, presented the College's case to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a>, which found the amendment of Dartmouth's charter to be an <a href="/wiki/Contract_Clause" title="Contract Clause">illegal impairment of a contract</a> by the state and reversed New Hampshire's takeover of the College. Webster concluded his <a href="/wiki/Peroration" title="Peroration">peroration</a> with the famous words: &quot;It is, Sir, as I have said, a small college. And yet there are those who love it.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-history-lesson_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-lesson-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Dartmouth emerged onto the national academic stage at the turn of the 20th century. Prior to this period, the College had clung to traditional methods of instruction and was relatively poorly funded.<sup id="cite_ref-trd-wheelock_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trd-wheelock-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Under the presidency of <a href="/wiki/William_Jewett_Tucker" title="William Jewett Tucker">William Jewett Tucker</a> (1893?1909), Dartmouth underwent a major revitalization of facilities, faculty, and the student body, following large endowments such as the $10,000 given by Dartmouth alumnus and law professor <a href="/wiki/John_Ordronaux_(doctor)" title="John Ordronaux (doctor)">John Ordronaux</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Many_Bequests_to_Carity.3B_Will_of_Dr._Ordronaux_D..._22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Many_Bequests_to_Carity.3B_Will_of_Dr._Ordronaux_D...-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> 20 new structures replaced antiquated buildings, while the student body and faculty both expanded threefold. Tucker is often credited for having &quot;refounded Dartmouth&quot; and bringing it into national prestige.<sup id="cite_ref-William_Jewett_Tucker_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-William_Jewett_Tucker-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_Hall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Dartmouth_Hall.jpg/220px-Dartmouth_Hall.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Dartmouth_Hall.jpg/330px-Dartmouth_Hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Dartmouth_Hall.jpg/440px-Dartmouth_Hall.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_Hall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Lithograph of the President's House, Thornton Hall, Dartmouth Hall, and Wentworth Hall, circa 1834.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Presidents <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Fox_Nichols" title="Ernest Fox Nichols">Ernest Fox Nichols</a> (1909?16) and <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Martin_Hopkins" title="Ernest Martin Hopkins">Ernest Martin Hopkins</a> (1916?45) continued Tucker's trend of modernization, further improving campus facilities and introducing <a href="/wiki/College_admissions_in_the_United_States" title="College admissions in the United States">selective admissions</a> in the 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-trd-wheelock_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trd-wheelock-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Sloan_Dickey" title="John Sloan Dickey">John Sloan Dickey</a>, serving as president from 1945 until 1970, strongly emphasized the liberal arts, particularly public policy and international relations.<sup id="cite_ref-trd-wheelock_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trd-wheelock-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-John_Sloan_Dickey_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_Sloan_Dickey-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Dartmouth was one of 131 colleges and universities nationally that took part in the <a href="/wiki/V-12_Navy_College_Training_Program" title="V-12 Navy College Training Program">V-12 Navy College Training Program</a> which offered students a path to a Navy commission.<sup id="cite_ref-navyarchives-v-12_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-navyarchives-v-12-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1970, longtime professor of <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> <a href="/wiki/John_George_Kemeny" title="John George Kemeny" class="mw-redirect">John George Kemeny</a> became president of Dartmouth.<sup id="cite_ref-kemeny-bio_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kemeny-bio-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> Kemeny oversaw several major changes at the College. Dartmouth, previously serving as a men's institution, began admitting women as full-time students and undergraduate degree candidates in 1972 amid much controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-When_did_Dartmouth_become_co-educational.3F_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-When_did_Dartmouth_become_co-educational.3F-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> At about the same time, the College adopted its &quot;<a href="#The_Dartmouth_Plan">Dartmouth Plan</a>&quot; of academic scheduling, permitting the student body to increase in size within the existing facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-kemeny-bio_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kemeny-bio-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>During the 1990s, the College saw a major academic overhaul under President <a href="/wiki/James_O._Freedman" title="James O. Freedman">James O. Freedman</a> and a controversial (and ultimately unsuccessful) 1999 initiative to encourage the school's single-sex Greek houses to go coed.<sup id="cite_ref-trd-wheelock_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trd-wheelock-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-James_O._Freedman_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James_O._Freedman-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> The first decade of the 21st century saw the commencement of the $1.3 billion Campaign for the Dartmouth Experience, the largest capital fundraising campaign in the College's history, which surpassed $1 billion in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-thedartmouth_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thedartmouth-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-thedartmouth1_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thedartmouth1-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> The mid- and late first decade of the 21st century have also seen extensive campus construction, with the erection of two new housing complexes, full renovation of two dormitories, and a forthcoming dining hall, life sciences center, and visual arts center.<sup id="cite_ref-Current_Capital_Projects_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Current_Capital_Projects-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2004, <a href="/wiki/Booz_Allen_Hamilton" title="Booz Allen Hamilton">Booz Allen Hamilton</a> selected Dartmouth College as a model of institutional endurance &quot;whose record of endurance has had implications and benefits for all American organizations, both academic and commercial,&quot; citing <i><a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College_v._Woodward" title="Dartmouth College v. Woodward">Trustees of Dartmouth College v. Woodward</a></i> and Dartmouth's successful self-reinvention in the late 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Booz_Allen_Hamilton_Lists_the_World.27s_Most_Enduring_Institutions_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Booz_Allen_Hamilton_Lists_the_World.27s_Most_Enduring_Institutions-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Since the election of a number of petition-nominated trustees to the <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Trustees_of_Dartmouth_College" title="Board of Trustees of Dartmouth College">Board of Trustees</a> starting in 2004, the role of alumni in Dartmouth governance has been the subject of ongoing conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-Battle_for_Board_leaves_boardroom_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Battle_for_Board_leaves_boardroom-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/James_Wright_(historian)" title="James Wright (historian)">James Wright</a> announced his retirement in February 2008<sup id="cite_ref-President_Wright_to_step_down_in_June_2009_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-President_Wright_to_step_down_in_June_2009-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> and was replaced by Harvard University professor and physician <a href="/wiki/Jim_Yong_Kim" title="Jim Yong Kim">Jim Yong Kim</a> on July 1, 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Dr._Jim_Yong_Kim_appointed_17th_President_of_Dartmouth_College_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dr._Jim_Yong_Kim_appointed_17th_President_of_Dartmouth_College-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In May 2010 Dartmouth joined the <a href="/wiki/Matariki_Network_of_Universities" title="Matariki Network of Universities">Matariki Network of Universities</a> (MNU) together with <a href="/wiki/Durham_University" title="Durham University">Durham University</a> (UK), <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_University" title="Queen's University">Queen's University</a> (Canada), <a href="/wiki/University_of_Otago" title="University of Otago">University of Otago</a> (New Zealand), <a href="/wiki/University_of_T%C3%BCbingen" title="University of T&uuml;bingen">University of T&uuml;bingen</a> (Germany), <a href="/wiki/University_of_Western_Australia" title="University of Western Australia">University of Western Australia</a> (Australia) and <a href="/wiki/Uppsala_University" title="Uppsala University">Uppsala University</a> (Sweden).<sup id="cite_ref-Members_of_the_Matariki_Network_of_Universities_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Members_of_the_Matariki_Network_of_Universities-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics.2C_administration.2C_and_ranking">Academics, administration, and ranking</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Dartmouth_College&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Academics, administration, and ranking">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <table class="infobox vcard" style="width: 22em">

        <td>113</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <th><i><a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education_World_University_Rankings" title="Times Higher Education World University Rankings">Times</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_THES_W_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_THES_W-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></th> 
        <td>124</td>

     </table> 
     <p>Dartmouth, a <a href="/wiki/Liberal_arts" title="Liberal arts" class="mw-redirect">liberal arts</a> institution, offers a four-year <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Arts" title="Bachelor of Arts">Bachelor of Arts</a> and ABET-accredited <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Engineering" title="Bachelor of Engineering">Bachelor of Engineering</a> degree to undergraduate students.<sup id="cite_ref-About_Dartmouth_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-About_Dartmouth-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-about-dartmouth-facts_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-about-dartmouth-facts-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> The college boasts 39 academic departments offering 56 <a href="/wiki/Academic_major" title="Academic major">major programs</a>, while students are free to design special majors or engage in dual majors.<sup id="cite_ref-Undergraduate_Majors_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Undergraduate_Majors-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2008, the most popular majors were economics, government, history, psychological and brain sciences, English, biology, and engineering sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-Dartmouth_Commencement_2008_Class_Notes_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth_Commencement_2008_Class_Notes-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> The Government Department, whose prominent professors include <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Brooks_(academic)" title="Stephen Brooks (academic)">Stephen Brooks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Ned_Lebow" title="Richard Ned Lebow">Richard Ned Lebow</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_Wohlforth" title="William Wohlforth">William Wohlforth</a>, was ranked the top solely undergraduate political science program in the world by researchers at the <a href="/wiki/London_School_of_Economics" title="London School of Economics">London School of Economics</a> in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-politicalstudies_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-politicalstudies-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> The Economics Department, whose prominent professors include <a href="/wiki/David_Blanchflower" title="David Blanchflower">David Blanchflower</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Samwick" title="Andrew Samwick">Andrew Samwick</a>, also holds the distinction as the top-ranked bachelor's-only economics program in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_of_Academic_Journals_and_Institutions_in_Economics_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_of_Academic_Journals_and_Institutions_in_Economics-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2012, Dartmouth was ranked tenth among undergraduate programs at national universities by <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-usnews-rankings_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usnews-rankings-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Dartmouth's strength in undergraduate education is highlighted by <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report</i> when in 2009, 2010, 2011, and 2012 it ranked Dartmouth first in undergraduate teaching at national universities.<sup id="cite_ref-usnews-rankings_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usnews-rankings-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> It also ranks first in High School Counselor Rankings as of 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-usnews-rankings_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usnews-rankings-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> The college ranks number seven in <i>The Wall Street Journal'</i>s ranking of top feeder schools.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ_Ranking_the_Colleges_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ_Ranking_the_Colleges-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> The 2006 <a href="/wiki/The_Carnegie_Foundation_for_the_Advancement_of_Teaching" title="The Carnegie Foundation for the Advancement of Teaching" class="mw-redirect">Carnegie Foundation</a> classification listed Dartmouth as the only &quot;majority-undergraduate&quot;, &quot;arts-and-sciences focus[ed]&quot;, &quot;research university&quot; in the country that also had &quot;some graduate coexistence&quot; and &quot;very high research activity.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-carnegiefoundation_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carnegiefoundation-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Carnegie_Classification_of_Institutions_of_Higher_Education_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Carnegie_Classification_of_Institutions_of_Higher_Education-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Classifications:_Dartmouth_College_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Classifications:_Dartmouth_College-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> Internationally, Dartmouth College was ranked 113th in the world in the 2012 <a href="/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings" title="QS World University Rankings">QS World University Rankings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-topuniversities_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-topuniversities-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2012, 23,110 students applied for approximately 1,100 places, and 9.4% of applicants were admitted. Of those admitted whose high schools reported a <a href="/wiki/Class_rank" title="Class rank">class rank</a>, 93.8% were ranked in the top 10% of their high school graduating class, 43.8% were valedictorians, and 12.7% were salutatorians. The mean <a href="/wiki/SAT" title="SAT">SAT</a> scores of admitted students by section were 736 for critical reading, 741 for math, and 743 for writing.<sup id="cite_ref-2015-admissions_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015-admissions-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Dartmouth meets 100% of students' demonstrated financial need in order to attend the College, and currently admits all students, including internationals, on a <a href="/wiki/Need-blind_admission" title="Need-blind admission">need-blind basis</a>. Beginning in the 2008?2009 academic year, Dartmouth instituted a new <a href="/wiki/Student_financial_aid" title="Student financial aid" class="mw-redirect">financial aid</a> policy extending need-blind admission to international students and replaced all <a href="/wiki/Student_loans_in_the_United_States" title="Student loans in the United States">student loans</a> with scholarships and grants. Students from families with a combined annual income of less than $75,000 are not charged any tuition.<sup id="cite_ref-Dartmouth_announces_new_financial_aid_initiative_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth_announces_new_financial_aid_initiative-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Financial_aid_program_kicks_off_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Financial_aid_program_kicks_off-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> However, in early 2010, the College announced that it would re-introduce loans to its financial aid packages beginning in the 2011?2012 school year due to its changed financial situation.<sup id="cite_ref-censky_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censky-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Most_Colleges_Plan_to_Stick_With_Pledges_to_Limit_Loans_in_Student_Aid_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Most_Colleges_Plan_to_Stick_With_Pledges_to_Limit_Loans_in_Student_Aid-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In order to graduate, a student must complete 35 total courses, eight to ten of which are typically part of a chosen major program.<sup id="cite_ref-requirements_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-requirements-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> Other requirements for graduation include the completion of ten &quot;distributive requirements&quot; in a variety of academic fields, proficiency in a foreign language, and completion of a writing class and first-year seminar in writing.<sup id="cite_ref-requirements_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-requirements-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> Many departments offer honors programs requiring students seeking that distinction to engage in &quot;independent, sustained work,&quot; culminating in the production of a <a href="/wiki/Thesis" title="Thesis">thesis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-requirements_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-requirements-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition to the courses offered in Hanover, Dartmouth offers 57 different off-campus programs, including Foreign Study Programs, Language Study Abroad programs, and Exchange Programs.<sup id="cite_ref-Programs.C2.A0.E2.80.93_List_All_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Programs.C2.A0.E2.80.93_List_All-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Types_of_Programs_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Types_of_Programs-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Through the Graduate Studies program, Dartmouth grants doctorate and master's degrees in 19 Arts &amp; Sciences graduate programs. Although the first graduate degree, a PhD in classics, was awarded in 1885, many of the current PhD programs have only existed since the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-about-dartmouth-facts_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-about-dartmouth-facts-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Furthermore, Dartmouth is home to three professional schools: the <a href="/wiki/Geisel_School_of_Medicine" title="Geisel School of Medicine">Geisel School of Medicine</a> (established 1797), <a href="/wiki/Thayer_School_of_Engineering" title="Thayer School of Engineering">Thayer School of Engineering</a> (1867)&nbsp;? which also serves as the undergraduate department of engineering sciences&nbsp;? and <a href="/wiki/Tuck_School_of_Business" title="Tuck School of Business">Tuck School of Business</a> (1900). With these professional schools and graduate programs, conventional American usage would accord Dartmouth the label of &quot;Dartmouth University&quot;;<sup id="cite_ref-about-dartmouth-facts_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-about-dartmouth-facts-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> however, because of historical and nostalgic reasons (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College_v._Woodward" title="Dartmouth College v. Woodward">Dartmouth College v. Woodward</a></i>), the school uses the name &quot;Dartmouth College&quot; to refer to the entire institution.<sup id="cite_ref-history-lesson_16-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-lesson-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Dartmouth employs a total of 607 tenured or tenure-track faculty members, including the highest proportion of female tenured professors among the Ivy League universities.<sup id="cite_ref-about-dartmouth-facts_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-about-dartmouth-facts-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Faculty members have been at the forefront of such major academic developments as the <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Conferences" title="Dartmouth Conferences">Dartmouth Conferences</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Time_Sharing_System" title="Dartmouth Time Sharing System">Dartmouth Time Sharing System</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_BASIC" title="Dartmouth BASIC">Dartmouth BASIC</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_ALGOL_30" title="Dartmouth ALGOL 30">Dartmouth ALGOL 30</a>. In 2005, sponsored project awards to Dartmouth faculty research amounted to $169 million.<sup id="cite_ref-Academics_.26_Research_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Academics_.26_Research-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Dartmouth serves as the host institution of the <a href="/wiki/University_Press_of_New_England" title="University Press of New England">University Press of New England</a>, a <a href="/wiki/University_press" title="University press" class="mw-redirect">university press</a> founded in 1970 that is supported by a consortium of schools that also includes Brandeis University, the University of New Hampshire, Northeastern University, Tufts University and the University of Vermont.<sup id="cite_ref-About_UPNE_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-About_UPNE-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="rellink boilerplate further">
      Further information: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Dartmouth_College_faculty" title="List of Dartmouth College faculty">List of Dartmouth College faculty</a>
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Dartmouth_Plan">The Dartmouth Plan</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Dartmouth_College&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The Dartmouth Plan">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:BakerLibrary.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/BakerLibrary.jpg/170px-BakerLibrary.jpg" width="170" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/BakerLibrary.jpg/255px-BakerLibrary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/BakerLibrary.jpg/340px-BakerLibrary.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:BakerLibrary.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Baker_Memorial_Library" title="Baker Memorial Library">Baker Memorial Library</a> at Dartmouth College
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Dartmouth functions on a quarter system, operating year-round on four ten-week <a href="/wiki/Academic_term" title="Academic term">academic terms</a>. The Dartmouth Plan (or simply &quot;D-Plan&quot;) is an academic scheduling system that permits the customization of each student's academic year. All undergraduates are required to be in residence for the fall, winter, and spring terms of their freshman and senior years, as well as the summer term of their sophomore year.<sup id="cite_ref-d-plan-admin_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-d-plan-admin-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> During all other terms, students are permitted to choose between studying on-campus, studying at an off-campus program, or taking a term off for vacation, outside internships, or research projects.<sup id="cite_ref-d-plan-admin_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-d-plan-admin-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> The typical course load is three classes per term, and students will generally enroll in classes for 12 total terms over the course of their academic career.<sup id="cite_ref-Working_Rules_and_Procedures_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Working_Rules_and_Procedures-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The D-Plan was instituted in the early 1970s at the same time that Dartmouth began accepting female undergraduates. It was initially devised as a plan to increase the enrollment without enlarging campus accommodations, and has been described as &quot;a way to put 4,000 students into 3,000 beds.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-trd-wheelock_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trd-wheelock-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Although new dormitories have been built since, the number of students has also increased and the D-Plan remains in effect. It was modified in the 1980s in an attempt to reduce the problems of lack of social and academic continuity.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Board_of_Trustees">Board of Trustees</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Dartmouth_College&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Board of Trustees">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Trustees_of_Dartmouth_College" title="Board of Trustees of Dartmouth College">Board of Trustees of Dartmouth College</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Dartmouth_Hall_02.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Dartmouth_Hall_02.JPG/220px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Dartmouth_Hall_02.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Dartmouth_Hall_02.JPG/330px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Dartmouth_Hall_02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Dartmouth_Hall_02.JPG/440px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Dartmouth_Hall_02.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Dartmouth_Hall_02.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Dartmouth Hall, built in 1784
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Dartmouth is governed by a Board of Trustees comprising the College president (<i>ex officio</i>), the state governor (<i>ex officio</i>), 13 trustees nominated and elected by the board (called &quot;charter trustees&quot;), and eight trustees nominated by alumni and elected by the board (&quot;alumni trustees&quot;).<sup id="cite_ref-Dartmouth_Trustees_vote_to_expand_size_of_board_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth_Trustees_vote_to_expand_size_of_board-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> The nominees for alumni trustee are determined by a poll of the members of the Association of Alumni of Dartmouth College, selecting from among names put forward by the Alumni Council or by alumni petition.</p> 
     <p>Although the Board elected its members from the two sources of nominees in equal proportions between 1891 and 2007,<sup id="cite_ref-Board_of_trustees_vote_to_change_how_Dartmouth_College_is_run_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Board_of_trustees_vote_to_change_how_Dartmouth_College_is_run-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> the Board decided in 2007 to add several new members, all charter trustees.<sup id="cite_ref-Board_adds_8_seats.2C_ends_century-old_parity_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Board_adds_8_seats.2C_ends_century-old_parity-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> In the controversy that followed the decision, the Association of Alumni filed a lawsuit, although it later withdrew the action.<sup id="cite_ref-Divided_Association_of_Alumni_sues_College_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Divided_Association_of_Alumni_sues_College-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-thedartmouth2_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thedartmouth2-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2008, the Board added five new charter trustees.<sup id="cite_ref-Dartmouth_College.27s_Board_of_Trustees_Elects_Five_Alumni_as_New_Trustees_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth_College.27s_Board_of_Trustees_Elects_Five_Alumni_as_New_Trustees-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Dartmouth_College&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Campus">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Dartmouth_College_buildings" title="List of Dartmouth College buildings" class="mw-redirect">List of Dartmouth College buildings</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width: 30%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;"> 
      <div style="position: relative; text-align: left;"> 
       <div> 
        <p>&quot;This is what a college is supposed to look like.&quot;</p> 
       </div> 
      </div> 
      <div style="text-align: left;">
       &nbsp;? 
       <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>, 1953
       <sup id="cite_ref-Remarks_at_the_Dartmouth_College_Commencement_Ceremony_in_Hanover.2C_New_Hampshire_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Remarks_at_the_Dartmouth_College_Commencement_Ceremony_in_Hanover.2C_New_Hampshire-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup>
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Dartmouth College is situated in the rural town of <a href="/wiki/Hanover,_New_Hampshire" title="Hanover, New Hampshire">Hanover, New Hampshire</a>, located in the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Valley_(Connecticut_River)" title="Upper Valley (Connecticut River)" class="mw-redirect">Upper Valley</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_River" title="Connecticut River">Connecticut River</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>. Its 269 acres (1.09&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) campus is centered on a five-acre (two-hectare) &quot;<a href="/wiki/The_Green_(Dartmouth_College)" title="The Green (Dartmouth College)">Green</a>&quot;,<sup id="cite_ref-The_Campus_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Campus-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> a former field of <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine trees</a> cleared by the College in 1771.<sup id="cite_ref-dartmo-green_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dartmo-green-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> Dartmouth is the largest private landowner of the town of Hanover,<sup id="cite_ref-Open_Space_Priorities_Plan_Summary_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Open_Space_Priorities_Plan_Summary-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> and its total landholdings and facilities are worth an estimated $434 million.<sup id="cite_ref-irs_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-irs-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition to its campus in Hanover, Dartmouth owns 4,500 acres (18&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Moosilauke" title="Mount Moosilauke">Mount Moosilauke</a> in the <a href="/wiki/White_Mountains_Region" title="White Mountains Region">White Mountains Region</a><sup id="cite_ref-dartmouth3_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dartmouth3-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> and a 27,000 acres (110&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) tract of land in northern New Hampshire known as the <a href="/wiki/Second_College_Grant,_New_Hampshire" title="Second College Grant, New Hampshire">Second College Grant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Second_College_Grant_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Second_College_Grant-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Dartmouth's campus buildings vary in age from Wentworth and Thornton Halls of the 1820s (the oldest surviving buildings constructed by the College) to new dormitories and mathematics facilities completed in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-Kemeny_Hall_and_Haldeman_Center_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemeny_Hall_and_Haldeman_Center-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McLaughlin_Cluster_Residence_Halls_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLaughlin_Cluster_Residence_Halls-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Most of Dartmouth's buildings are designed in the <a href="/wiki/Georgian_architecture" title="Georgian architecture">Georgian</a> <a href="/wiki/American_colonial_architecture" title="American colonial architecture">American colonial</a> style,<sup id="cite_ref-CIC_Historic_Campus_Architecture_Project_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIC_Historic_Campus_Architecture_Project-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Atkin_Olshin_Lawson-Bell_Architects_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atkin_Olshin_Lawson-Bell_Architects-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-A_History_of_American_Higher_Education_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_History_of_American_Higher_Education-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> a theme which has been preserved in recent architectural additions.<sup id="cite_ref-Dartmouth_Landscape_Design_Guidelines_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth_Landscape_Design_Guidelines-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> The College has actively sought to reduce carbon emissions and energy usage on campus, earning it the grade of A- from the Sustainable Endowments Institute on its College Sustainability Report Card 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-Dartmouth_Sustainability_Initiative_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth_Sustainability_Initiative-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-College_Sustainability_Report_Card_2008_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-College_Sustainability_Report_Card_2008-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Academic_facilities">Academic facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Dartmouth_College&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Academic facilities">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Hopkins_Center_for_the_Arts,_Dartmouth_College.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Hopkins_Center_for_the_Arts%2C_Dartmouth_College.jpg/220px-Hopkins_Center_for_the_Arts%2C_Dartmouth_College.jpg" width="220" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Hopkins_Center_for_the_Arts%2C_Dartmouth_College.jpg/330px-Hopkins_Center_for_the_Arts%2C_Dartmouth_College.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Hopkins_Center_for_the_Arts%2C_Dartmouth_College.jpg/440px-Hopkins_Center_for_the_Arts%2C_Dartmouth_College.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Hopkins_Center_for_the_Arts,_Dartmouth_College.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Hopkins Center
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The College's creative and performing arts facility is the <a href="/wiki/Hopkins_Center_for_the_Arts" title="Hopkins Center for the Arts">Hopkins Center for the Arts</a> (&quot;the Hop&quot;). Opened in 1962, the Hop houses the College's drama, music, film, and studio arts departments, as well as a woodshop, pottery studio, and jewelry studio which are open for use by students and faculty.<sup id="cite_ref-hop-info_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hop-info-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> The building was designed by the famed architect <a href="/wiki/Wallace_Harrison" title="Wallace Harrison">Wallace Harrison</a>, who would later design the similar-looking fa&ccedil;ade of <a href="/wiki/City_of_New_York" title="City of New York" class="mw-redirect">Manhattan's</a> <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Opera" title="Metropolitan Opera">Metropolitan Opera House</a> at <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Center" title="Lincoln Center" class="mw-redirect">Lincoln Center</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Hopkins_Center_Turns_40_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Hopkins_Center_Turns_40-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Its facilities include two theaters and one 900-seat auditorium.<sup id="cite_ref-hop-info_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hop-info-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> The Hop is also the location of all student mailboxes (&quot;Hinman boxes&quot;)<sup id="cite_ref-Undergraduate_Student_Mail_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Undergraduate_Student_Mail-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> and the Courtyard Caf&eacute; dining facility.<sup id="cite_ref-Dining_Locations:_Courtyard_Caf.C3.A9_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dining_Locations:_Courtyard_Caf.C3.A9-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> The Hop is connected to the <a href="/wiki/Hood_Museum_of_Art" title="Hood Museum of Art">Hood Museum of Art</a>, arguably North America's oldest museum in continuous operation,<sup id="cite_ref-Dartmouth_College:_Services_and_Facilities_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth_College:_Services_and_Facilities-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> and the Loew Auditorium, where films are screened.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Arts_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Arts-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-20_09.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-20_09.JPG/220px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-20_09.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-20_09.JPG/330px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-20_09.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-20_09.JPG/440px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-20_09.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-20_09.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> A view of the Sherman Fairchild Physical Science Center and Wheeler Hall from the tower of 
        <a href="/wiki/Baker_Memorial_Library" title="Baker Memorial Library">Baker Memorial Library</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In addition to its 19 graduate programs in the arts and sciences, Dartmouth is home to three separate graduate schools. <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Medical_School" title="Dartmouth Medical School" class="mw-redirect">Dartmouth Medical School</a> is located in a complex on the north side of campus<sup id="cite_ref-Maps_and_Directions_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maps_and_Directions-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> and includes laboratories, classrooms, offices, and a biomedical library.<sup id="cite_ref-d-maps_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-d-maps-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth-Hitchcock_Medical_Center" title="Dartmouth-Hitchcock Medical Center" class="mw-redirect">Dartmouth-Hitchcock Medical Center</a>, located several miles to the south in <a href="/wiki/Lebanon,_New_Hampshire" title="Lebanon, New Hampshire">Lebanon, New Hampshire</a>, contains a 396-bed <a href="/wiki/Teaching_hospital" title="Teaching hospital">teaching hospital</a> for the Medical School.<sup id="cite_ref-About_Dartmouth-Hitchcock_Medical_Center_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-About_Dartmouth-Hitchcock_Medical_Center-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Thayer_School_of_Engineering" title="Thayer School of Engineering">Thayer School of Engineering</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tuck_School_of_Business" title="Tuck School of Business">Tuck School of Business</a> are both located at the end of Tuck Mall, west of the center of campus and near the Connecticut River.<sup id="cite_ref-d-maps_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-d-maps-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> The Thayer School presently comprises two buildings;<sup id="cite_ref-d-maps_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-d-maps-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> Tuck has seven academic and administrative buildings, as well as several common areas.<sup id="cite_ref-tuck-campus_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tuck-campus-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> The two graduate schools share a library, the Feldberg Business &amp; Engineering Library.<sup id="cite_ref-tuck-campus_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tuck-campus-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Dartmouth's nine libraries are all part of the collective Dartmouth College Library, which comprises 2.48 million volumes and 6 million total resources, including videos, maps, sound recordings, and photographs.<sup id="cite_ref-about-dartmouth-facts_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-about-dartmouth-facts-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Library_Holdings_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Library_Holdings-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> Its specialized libraries include the Biomedical Libraries, Evans Map Room, Feldberg Business &amp; Engineering Library, Jones Media Center, Kresge Physical Sciences Library, Paddock Music Library, Rauner Special Collections Library, and Sherman Art Library. Baker-Berry Library is the main library at Dartmouth, comprising <a href="/wiki/Baker_Memorial_Library" title="Baker Memorial Library">Baker Memorial Library</a> (opened 1928) and Berry Library (opened 2000<sup id="cite_ref-Berry_Library_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berry_Library-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup>). Located on the northern side of the Green, Baker's 200-foot (61&nbsp;m) tower is an iconic symbol of the College.<sup id="cite_ref-Baker_Library_Bell_Tower_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker_Library_Bell_Tower-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-No_Bridge_Left_Unburned:_Rage_at_Dartmouth_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-No_Bridge_Left_Unburned:_Rage_at_Dartmouth-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nhspe_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhspe-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Athletic_facilities">Athletic facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Dartmouth_College&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Athletic facilities">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Memorial_Field_02.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Memorial_Field_02.JPG/175px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Memorial_Field_02.JPG" width="175" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Memorial_Field_02.JPG/263px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Memorial_Field_02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Memorial_Field_02.JPG/350px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Memorial_Field_02.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Memorial_Field_02.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Field_(Dartmouth_College)" title="Memorial Field (Dartmouth College)" class="mw-redirect">Memorial Field</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Dartmouth's original sports field was <a href="/wiki/The_Green_(Dartmouth_College)" title="The Green (Dartmouth College)">the Green</a>, where students played <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_division_football" title="Old division football">old division football</a> during the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-dartmo-green_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dartmo-green-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> Today, two of Dartmouth's athletic facilities are located in the southeast corner of campus.<sup id="cite_ref-dcaf_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dcaf-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> The center of athletic life is the <a href="/wiki/Alumni_Gymnasium_(Dartmouth_College)" title="Alumni Gymnasium (Dartmouth College)">Alumni Gymnasium</a>, which includes the Karl Michael Competition Pool and the Spaulding Pool, a state of the art fitness center, a weight room, and a 1/13th-mile (123&nbsp;m) indoor track.<sup id="cite_ref-Alumni_Gym_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alumni_Gym-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> Attached to Alumni Gymnasium is the Berry Sports Center, which contains basketball and volleyball courts (<a href="/wiki/Leede_Arena" title="Leede Arena">Leede Arena</a>), as well as the Kresge Fitness Center.<sup id="cite_ref-Berry_Sports_Center_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berry_Sports_Center-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> Behind the Alumni Gymnasium is <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Field_(Dartmouth_College)" title="Memorial Field (Dartmouth College)" class="mw-redirect">Memorial Field</a>, a 15,600-seat stadium overlooking Dartmouth's football field and track.<sup id="cite_ref-Memorial_Field_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Memorial_Field-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> The nearby <a href="/wiki/Thompson_Arena" title="Thompson Arena">Thompson Arena</a>, designed by Italian engineer <a href="/wiki/Pier_Luigi_Nervi" title="Pier Luigi Nervi">Pier Luigi Nervi</a> and constructed in 1975, houses Dartmouth's ice rink.<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson_Arena_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson_Arena-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> Also visible from Memorial Field is the 91,800-square-foot (8,530&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) Nathaniel Leverone Fieldhouse, home to the indoor track. The new softball field, Dartmouth Softball Park, was constructed in 2012, sharing parking facilities with Thompson arena.</p> 
     <p>Dartmouth's other athletic facilities in Hanover include the Friends of Dartmouth Rowing Boathouse and the old rowing house storage facility (both located along the Connecticut River), the <a href="/wiki/Hanover_Country_Club" title="Hanover Country Club">Hanover Country Club</a>, Dartmouth's oldest remaining athletic facility (established in 1899),<sup id="cite_ref-History_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> and the Corey Ford Rugby Clubhouse.<sup id="cite_ref-Rugby_Fires_It_Up_With_New_Clubhouse_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rugby_Fires_It_Up_With_New_Clubhouse-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> The College also maintains the <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Skiway" title="Dartmouth Skiway">Dartmouth Skiway</a>, a 100 acres (0.40&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) skiing facility located over two mountains near the Hanover campus in <a href="/wiki/Lyme_Center,_New_Hampshire" title="Lyme Center, New Hampshire">Lyme Center, New Hampshire</a><sup id="cite_ref-Dartmouth_Skiway_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth_Skiway-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> that serves as the winter practice grounds for the nationally dominant Dartmouth ski team.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Housing_and_student_life_facilities">Housing and student life facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Dartmouth_College&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Housing and student life facilities">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Lord_Hall.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Lord_Hall.JPG/220px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Lord_Hall.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Lord_Hall.JPG/330px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Lord_Hall.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Lord_Hall.JPG/440px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Lord_Hall.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-06-23_Lord_Hall.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Lord Hall in the Gold Coast Cluster
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Instead of ungrouped dormitories or <a href="/wiki/Residential_colleges" title="Residential colleges" class="mw-redirect">residential colleges</a>, Dartmouth has nine residential communities located throughout campus.<sup id="cite_ref-orl_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-orl-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> The dormitories vary in design from modern to traditional Georgian styles, and room arrangements range from singles to quads and apartment suites.<sup id="cite_ref-orl_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-orl-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> Since 2006, the College has guaranteed housing for students during their freshman and sophomore years.<sup id="cite_ref-Assembly_reworks_UFC_membership_guidelines_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Assembly_reworks_UFC_membership_guidelines-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> More than 3,000 students elect to live in housing provided by College.<sup id="cite_ref-orl_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-orl-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Campus meals are served by Dartmouth Dining Services, which operates 11 dining establishments around campus.<sup id="cite_ref-Campus_Map_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campus_Map-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> Four of them are located at the center of campus in Thayer Dining Hall.<sup id="cite_ref-Dining_Locations_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dining_Locations-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The Collis Center is the center of student life and programming, serving as what would be generically termed the &quot;student union&quot; or &quot;campus center.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-Collis_Center_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collis_Center-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> It contains a caf&eacute;, study space, common areas, and a number of administrative departments, including the <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College_Academic_Skills_Center" title="Dartmouth College Academic Skills Center">Academic Skills Centre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Collis_Floor_Plans_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collis_Floor_Plans-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Administrative_Departments_in_Collis_Center_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Administrative_Departments_in_Collis_Center-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> Robinson Hall, next door to both Collis and Thayer, contains the offices of a number of student organizations including the <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Outing_Club" title="Dartmouth Outing Club">Dartmouth Outing Club</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dartmouth" title="The Dartmouth">The Dartmouth</a></i> daily newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-Robinson_Hall_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson_Hall-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_life">Student life</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Dartmouth_College&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Student life">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <p>In 2006, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Princeton_Review" title="The Princeton Review">The Princeton Review</a></i> ranked Dartmouth third in its &quot;Quality of Life&quot; category, and sixth for having the &quot;Happiest Students.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-Best_361_Colleges_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Best_361_Colleges-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> Athletics and participation in the Greek system are the most popular campus activities;<sup id="cite_ref-abc-greek_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abc-greek-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> in all, Dartmouth offers more than 350 organizations, teams, and sports.<sup id="cite_ref-Student_Life_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Student_Life-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> The school is also home to a variety of longstanding traditions and celebrations.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_groups">Student groups</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Dartmouth_College&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Student groups">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles: 
      <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College_student_groups" title="Dartmouth College student groups">Dartmouth College student groups</a>, 
      <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College_publications" title="Dartmouth College publications">Dartmouth College publications</a>, and 
      <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College_Greek_organizations" title="Dartmouth College Greek organizations">Dartmouth College Greek organizations</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-03_Robinson_Hall.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-03_Robinson_Hall.JPG/220px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-03_Robinson_Hall.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-03_Robinson_Hall.JPG/330px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-03_Robinson_Hall.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-03_Robinson_Hall.JPG/440px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-03_Robinson_Hall.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-10-03_Robinson_Hall.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Robinson Hall houses many of the College's student-run organizations, including the 
        <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Outing_Club" title="Dartmouth Outing Club">Dartmouth Outing Club</a>. The building is a designated stop along the 
        <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Trail" title="Appalachian Trail">Appalachian Trail</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Dartmouth's more than 200 student organizations and clubs cover a wide range of interests.<sup id="cite_ref-Campus_Life:_Clubs_and_Organizations_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campus_Life:_Clubs_and_Organizations-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2007, the College hosted eight academic groups, 17 cultural groups, two honor societies, 30 &quot;issue-oriented&quot; groups, 25 performing groups, 12 pre-professional groups, 20 publications, and 11 recreational groups.<sup id="cite_ref-COSO_Student_Organizations_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-COSO_Student_Organizations-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> Notable student groups include the nation's largest and oldest collegiate outdoors club, the <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_Outing_Club" title="Dartmouth Outing Club">Dartmouth Outing Club</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-DOCalumnimag_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOCalumnimag-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> which includes the nationally recognized<sup id="cite_ref-Big_Green_Bus_in_the_News_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Big_Green_Bus_in_the_News-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Green_Bus" title="The Big Green Bus">Big Green Bus</a>, the campus's oldest and most prestigious a cappella group, <a href="/wiki/The_Dartmouth_Aires" title="The Dartmouth Aires">The Dartmouth Aires</a>, the controversial conservative newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dartmouth_Review" title="The Dartmouth Review">The Dartmouth Review</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Reagan.27s_Disappearing_Bureaucrats_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reagan.27s_Disappearing_Bureaucrats-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dartmouth" title="The Dartmouth">The Dartmouth</a></i>, arguably the nation's oldest university newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-nlestk_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nlestk-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>The Dartmouth</i> describes itself as &quot;America's Oldest College Newspaper, Founded 1799.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-nlestk_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nlestk-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Partially because of Dartmouth's rural, isolated location, the <a href="/wiki/Fraternities_and_sororities" title="Fraternities and sororities" class="mw-redirect">Greek system</a> dating from the 1840s is one of the most popular social outlets for students.<sup id="cite_ref-abc-greek_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abc-greek-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Halls.2C_Tombs_and_Houses:_Student_Society_Architecture_at_Dartmouth_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halls.2C_Tombs_and_Houses:_Student_Society_Architecture_at_Dartmouth-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> Dartmouth is home to 32 recognized Greek houses: 17 fraternities, 12 sororities, and three coeducational organizations,<sup id="cite_ref-Coed.2C_Fraternity.2C_and_Sorority_Administration_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coed.2C_Fraternity.2C_and_Sorority_Administration-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2007, roughly 70% of eligible students belong to a Greek organization;<sup id="cite_ref-Transgenders_try_to_navigate_Greek_system_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenders_try_to_navigate_Greek_system-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> since 1987, students have not been permitted to join Greek organizations until their sophomore year.<sup id="cite_ref-History_of_CFS_Organizations_at_Dartmouth_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_CFS_Organizations_at_Dartmouth-129"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> Dartmouth College was among the first institutions of higher education to <a href="/wiki/Desegregate" title="Desegregate" class="mw-redirect">desegregate</a> fraternity houses in the 1950s, and was involved in the movement to create <a href="/wiki/Coeducational" title="Coeducational" class="mw-redirect">coeducational</a> Greek houses in the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-The_College_on_the_Hill:_A_Dartmouth_Chronicle_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_College_on_the_Hill:_A_Dartmouth_Chronicle-130"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> In the early first decade of the 21st century, campus-wide debate focused on a Board of Trustees recommendation that Greek organizations become &quot;substantially coeducational&quot;;<sup id="cite_ref-Dartmouth_to_Abolish_Fraternities_and_Sororities_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dartmouth_to_Abolish_Fraternities_and_Sororities-131"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup> this attempt to the change the Greek system eventually failed.<sup id="cite_ref-Interrogating_the_S.L.I._132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Interrogating_the_S.L.I.-132"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> The College has an additional classification of social/residential organizations known as <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College_student_groups#Undergraduate_societies" title="Dartmouth College student groups">undergraduate societies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Senior_and_Undergraduate_Society_Administration_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Senior_and_Undergraduate_Society_Administration-133"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The fraternities have an extensive history of <a href="/wiki/Hazing" title="Hazing">hazing</a> and alcohol abuse, leading to police raids and accusations of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_harassment" title="Sexual harassment">sexual harassment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
